    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="7c10b84d833e42969a4f8c5271fa0b88">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '4b4c1392-f17f-4ef4-b4f9-0cf5f315e8fd'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d93665360" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Fe-based sulfate materials have attracted much attention in the cathode of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to their low cost and high operating voltage, as well as possessing application prospects comparable to hard carbon in the anode based on Fe<sup>0</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> redox properties. However, the poor conductivity and the tendency to agglomerate have limited their further application. Herein, the study constructs a dual-conductive structure that the Na<sub>2.31</sub>Fe<sub>1.72</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> particles with amorphous carbon in situ-coated embedded in ketjen black (KB) conducting carbon networks (NFS/KB) via a solid phase ball-milling strategy, which effectively enhanced inherent conductivity and electron transfer efficiency among particles of the Fe-based sulphate. The NFS/KB can provide a reversible capacity of 92 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C and stable cycling stability at high current (85% retention after 500 cycles at 20 C) as cathode of SIBs. Surprisingly, as anode of SIBs, the NFS/KB delivers a rate performance (149 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C) that is superior to that of hard carbon. Notably, further application of the material to symmetric SIBs also achieves favorable results. This work effectively enhances the performance of Fe-based sulphate materials in SIBs with a low-cost carbon modification method, providing a new approach for the low-cost symmetric SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202402145-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202402145-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium ion batteries (SIBs) with low cost, abundant sodium resources, and similar operating mechanism to lithium-ion batteries have become potential candidates in the field of electrochemical energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0001_R_d93665352e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Curr. Opin. Solid State Mater. Sci">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0006_R_d93665352e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Electrochem. Energy Rev.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a critical factor in SIBs, the cathode and anode materials determine the energy density, cycle stability, and safety of the battery system.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0007_R_d93665352e439" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Mater. Today">7</a>-<a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0012_R_d93665352e439" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At present, research on cathode materials for SIBs is focused on transition metal oxides,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0013_R_d93665352e444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Electrochem. Energy Rev.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Prussian blue analogs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0014_R_d93665352e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Mater. Today.">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0015_R_d93665352e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Carbon Neutralization">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and polyanionic compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0016_R_d93665352e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0017_R_d93665352e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Carbon Neutralization">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Of these, polyanionic compounds are of great interest due to their excellent rate, cycling, and safety performance. For anode materials, hard carbon has been widely used as SIBs anode material due to its good capacity, low redox potential, and excellent cycling stability, yet it suffers from safety issues (dendrite formation) and poor rate performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0018_R_d93665352e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">18</a>-<a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0020_R_d93665352e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Electrochem. Energy Rev.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the Fe-based polyanion sulphates with Fe<sup>0</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> as the redox center makes it highly attractive and promising for application in the anode and cathode of SIBs. Therefore, the Fe-based sulphates with abundant elemental reserves and multi redox center make it a promising advanced electrode material for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0021_R_d93665352e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Energy Chem.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>However, the poor thermal stability, low electronic conductivity, and environmental sensitivity of Fe-based sulphate materials make it difficult to utilize them effectively as electrode materials for SIBs. Some different modifications have been explored to enhance their performance, such as polyanions to increase their voltage plateaus,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0022_R_d93665352e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Small">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> elemental doping to boost their electrical conductivity,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0023_R_d93665352e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and nanosizing to improve their structural stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0024_R_d93665352e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, based on the physical nature of Fe-based sulphate, it is susceptible to agglomeration during the synthesis process, which makes it difficult to be nanosized. Normally, carbon modification is an effective way to improve the electrochemical properties of Fe-based sulphate materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0025_R_d93665352e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Power Sources">25</a>, <a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0026_R_d93665352e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Chem. Sci.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The carbon coating mainly enhances the electron transfer between the particles, contributing to their rate performance. And part of the carbon source can act as a reducing agent to prevent Fe<sup>2+</sup> from being oxidized, thus reducing impurities. Nevertheless, the homogeneous compounding of carbon materials with Fe-based sulphates by suitable methods is still challenging.</p>
         
         <p>Some different homogeneous carbon modification methods have been applied to enhance the performance of polyanionic electrode materials. It has been reported that homogeneous carbon-coated structures were prepared by sol-gel method with a two-step introduction of carbon source,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0027_R_d93665352e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nanoscale">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> but the multi-step synthesis means that the preparation process is more tedious. Besides, hydrothermal methods have also been used to achieve uniform carbon coverage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0028_R_d93665352e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the water-absorbing nature of the Fe-based sulphate leads to a higher risk of impurities and agglomeration during the preparation of the material by the high-temperature liquid-phase method described above. Moreover, the composites synthesized above are uniformly carbon coated, which does not improve the electronic transport between particles of Fe-based sulfates. However, the carbon coating structure based on a continuous conductive carbon network can increase the electronic conductivity of the material as well as the transport efficiency of ions among particles. Therefore, exploring the suitable design of dual-conductive modification becomes the key to improve the electrochemical performance of Fe-based sulfate materials.</p>
         
         <p>The low-temperature solid-phase methods prevent material from agglomeration because of slow diffusion process, which has become an effective way for carbon modification of Fe-based sulfate materials. Herein, with the large specific surface area and good dispersion of ketjen black, we have prepared composite of NFS/KB that the Na<sub>2.31</sub>Fe<sub>1.71</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> particles with amorphous carbon in situ-coated embedded in continuous ketjen black networks via a facile one-step solid phase ball-milling method. Based on the dual-conductive structure, the material exhibits excellent performance in both cathode and anode of SIBs. As cathode of SIBs, the NFS/KB provides a reversible capacity of 92 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C with a capacity retention rate of 85% after 500 cycles at 20 C. For anode, the NFS/KB shows a capacity of 160 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles at 2 C that surpasses even hard carbon anode of SIBs. More significantly, the symmetric sodium ion full cell assembled of the NFS/KB as both cathode and anode also exhibits good electrochemical performance, providing a reversible capacity of 75 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 C. This work effectively enhances the performance of Fe-based sulphate materials through simple synthesis method and the construction of the dual-carbon structure and is successfully applied to SIBs both cathode and anode, providing an advanced view of the commercialization applications of low-cost SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202402145-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202402145-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>A schematic representation of the synthesis of the sample is shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0001">1</a>. The target precursors were prepared by a facile one-step solid phase ball milling method (the details of the preparation method are provided in the supporting information). The prepared compounds are named NFS/C (NFS/AB with the addition of acetylene black, NFS/KB with the addition of ketjen black and NFS/SP with the addition of Super P). Obviously, the different conductive carbons show different particle sizes and degrees of dispersion. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), acetylene black (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a>, Supporting Information) and Super P (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1c</a>, Supporting Information) are both show various degrees of agglomeration, while ketjen black (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a>, Supporting Information) particles are relatively dispersed. Furthermore, the TEM images demonstrate that ketjen black (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1e</a>, Supporting Information) possesses a smaller particle size (10-20&nbsp;nm) and a unique network structure compared to acetylene black (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1d</a>, Supporting Information) and Super P (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1f</a>, Supporting Information), which facilitates the carbon material to cross-link and wrap the active particles to form a highly conductive network. In addition, the Nitrogen isothermal adsorption-desorption analyses show that ketjen black possesses a larger specific surface area (1464 m<sup>2</sup>/g) as well as a rich pore structure (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) shows the different results presented by various conductive carbon compounded with Fe-based sulfate materials (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0002">2</a>). Compared with NFS/AB (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0002">2a</a>) and NFS/SP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0002">2c</a>), NFS/KB (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0002">2b</a>) exhibits a more uniform and continuous 3D structure, which facilitates the electrolyte infiltration and ion transport. In order to further understand the influence of different carbon sources on coating uniformity, FTIR spectra of KB, SP and AB are provided and shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). The vibration peaks located at 1590 and 1250 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponding to -COOH and -OH can be detected in all the carbon sources. The FTIR analysis of KB reveals an increased intensity in the stretching vibration peaks of -COOH and -OH in comparison to AB and SP. These rich hydrophilic functional groups facilitate the uniform mixing of FeSO<sub>4</sub>·7H<sub>2</sub>O and ascorbic acid, allowing the active particles with smaller size homogeneous embed into the larger amorphous carbon layer after carbonization.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202402145-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e78d373c-128f-4d28-baf3-c766f4f94e33/adfm202402145-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e78d373c-128f-4d28-baf3-c766f4f94e33/adfm202402145-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bbcb2e3a-6fcb-4685-9ba2-af7be8186985/adfm202402145-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e78d373c-128f-4d28-baf3-c766f4f94e33/adfm202402145-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202402145-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202402145" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of the synthesis of NFS/C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202402145-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cd23d4d9-d48f-46ca-aeb8-8ff74f950410/adfm202402145-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cd23d4d9-d48f-46ca-aeb8-8ff74f950410/adfm202402145-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9fd3b6e7-ded5-4b02-b05a-5734b9d50e29/adfm202402145-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cd23d4d9-d48f-46ca-aeb8-8ff74f950410/adfm202402145-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202402145-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202402145" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images of a) NFS/AB, b) NFS/KB, and c) NFS/SP. TEM and HRTEM images of d–g) NFS/AB, e–h) NFS/KB and f–i) NFS/SP.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Furthermore, the transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0002">2d–f</a>) demonstrate that the morphologies correspond well to the SEM testing, which shows that the NFS/AB particles are severely agglomerated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0002">2d</a>), and the conductive carbon cannot be uniformly dispersed, making it difficult to form an effective carbon conductive network structure. And the NFS/SP showed that the active materials could not be uniformly dispersed in the carbon network (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0002">2f</a>), resulting in the lack of sufficient ion diffusion channels. While the NFS/KB is clearly interspersed with bright and dark particles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0002">2e</a>), the bright ones are conductive carbon and the dark ones are active particles, which can be seen that the active particles are uniformly dispersed in the continuous conductive network.</p>
         
         <p>The high-resolution TEM images of NFS/C are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0002">2g–i</a>. Because of the slow diffusion and anti-oxidation effect of ascorbic acid during synthesis, the active particles of all samples were coated with amorphous carbon. Benefiting from the well uniformity of ketjen black particles in the solid phase reaction can promote the dispersion of ascorbic acid, which makes NFS/KB (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0002">2h</a>) exhibit a thicker and uniform amorphous carbon layer. Owing to the well-constructed dual-carbon structure, the intrinsic conductivity and ion transport efficiency among particles of NFS are greatly improved. Besides, all samples have distinct lattice stripes with lattice spacing d = 0.250, 0.238, and 0.256&nbsp;nm corresponding to (−422), (−151), and (−132) crystal planes of Fe-based sulphate in the NFS/AB (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0002">2g</a>), NFS/KB (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0002">2h</a>) and NFS/SP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0002">2i</a>), respectively. Further, the STEM-EDS mapping results were carried out on the NFS/KB and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information) shows that the elements Fe, Na, S, O, and C are uniformly distributed, indicating a good degree of mixing. And the Na element is homogeneously mixed with the C element, further proving that the active substance in this material can be homogeneously compounded with the carbon material to form a uniform network structure.</p>
         
         <p>The X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of NFS/C are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0003">3a</a>. Based on the testing results, all samples exhibit similar peak patterns. However, due to the Fe-based sulfates phase usually possesses an inherent deviation from stoichiometry, leading to Na<sub>2+2x</sub>Fe<sub>2-x</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> compound, we further refined the XRD patterns of the three samples to determine their composition. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0003">3b–d</a>, the refined molecular formulas of NFS/KB, NFS/SP, and NFS/AB are Na<sub>2.31</sub>Fe<sub>1.72</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>2.34</sub>Fe<sub>1.63</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, and Na<sub>2.32</sub>Fe<sub>1.53</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, respectively. Compared with the feeding of iron, the small loss of iron in the bulk phase is mainly due to the presence of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> heterophase. According to the results of XRD refinement, the small content of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> in NFS/KB, NFS/SP, and NFS/AB samples is 2.59%, 3.68%, and 5.22%, respectively. The lowest content of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> in NFS/KB indicates that KB as a conductive carbon source is more conducive to the uniform mixing of precursor raw materials. In addition, the measured lattice parameters of NFS/KB are also in agreement with those reported previously in the article,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0029_R_d93665352e736" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nat. Commun.">29</a>, <a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0030_R_d93665352e736" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Energy Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> demonstrating a typical alluaudite phase (Tables&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1–S3</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202402145-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/55fac573-70f4-4040-a3cc-176402e45576/adfm202402145-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/55fac573-70f4-4040-a3cc-176402e45576/adfm202402145-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9b511868-41ac-45ab-ad31-310f990ba3c7/adfm202402145-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/55fac573-70f4-4040-a3cc-176402e45576/adfm202402145-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202402145-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202402145" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns and b–d) corresponding Rietveld refinements of NFS/KB, NFS/SP, and NFS/AB.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The XRD patterns of NFS/KB, NFS/SP, and NFS/AB have been marked for comparison, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0003">3</a>. All samples possess high crystallinity with a space group of C 2/c. Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information) demonstrates that the (240) diffraction peak of NFS/KB shifts toward a lower angle compared to other samples, indicating an increase in cell volume, which is consistent with their XRD refine results. A larger cell volume will reduce the resistance of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction from the lattice. Rietveld refinements of XRD of the three samples with reliability factors (X<sup>2</sup> = 2.59 for the NFS/KB, X<sup>2</sup> = 4.37 for the NFS/AB and X<sup>2</sup> = 4.61 for the NFS/SP) are shown in Tables&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1–S3</a> (Supporting Information), according to their detailed structural information, the presence of Na1 in NFS/KB is greater than in the other two samples, suggesting that the NFS/KB sample possesses a more stable structural framework.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0031_R_d93665352e784" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Mater. Chem. A">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the presence of Na2 in NFS/KB is also larger than in the other two samples, indicating that Na<sup>+</sup> is more prone to de/insertion during charging and discharging.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0032_R_d93665352e792" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Adv. Funct. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To further identify the accurate molecular formula, the three samples were verified by inductively coupled plasma-atomic emission spectrometry (ICP-AES). As shown in Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information), the ratio of Na:Fe:S in all the samples is ≈2.2:1.8:3, basically consistent with that anticipated from the starting composition.</p>
         
         <p>Raman spectra of NFS/C are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0004">4a</a>, the two typical characteristic peaks near 1350 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 1590 cm<sup>−1</sup> for all samples correspond to D band (disordered carbon, <i>sp<sup>3</sup></i> hybrid) and G band (graphitic carbon, <i>sp<sup>2</sup></i> hybrid) of carbon,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0033_R_d93665352e822" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Phys. Chem. C">33</a>-<a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0036_R_d93665352e822" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Chem Comm.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively. And the ID:IG ratios of NFS/AB, NFS/KB, and NFS/SP are 0.8, 0.85, and 0.82, respectively. The NFS/KB exhibits the highest degree of carbon disorder, representing its higher degree of carbon defects, which facilitates the electrolyte to fully infiltrate the active material. The FT-IR spectra of the NFS/C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0004">4b</a>) exhibit a typical SO<sub>4</sub><sup>2−</sup> group distributed between 1200 and 400 cm<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0037_R_d93665352e836" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Mater. Chem. A.">37</a>, <a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0038_R_d93665352e836" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, broader of all samples at positions ≈3400 cm<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding mainly to the (-OH) symmetric/asymmetric stretching vibrations of the H<sub>2</sub>O unit.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0039_R_d93665352e846" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared to the NFS/AB and NFS/SP, the NFS/KB exhibits weaker peaks, suggesting that the uniform and continuous carbon wrapping is effective in reducing the effect of moisture on the material.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202402145-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/101e06c9-9557-4b7d-b61e-1c602da7334f/adfm202402145-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/101e06c9-9557-4b7d-b61e-1c602da7334f/adfm202402145-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/85d40f51-a815-4b05-ab6b-65e51080501c/adfm202402145-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/101e06c9-9557-4b7d-b61e-1c602da7334f/adfm202402145-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202402145-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202402145" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) Raman and FT-IR spectra of the NFS/C. c) Full XPS spectrum, d) C1s characteristic spectra, and e) Fe2p fitting graphs of NFS/KB.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The full XPS spectrum of the NFS/KB with typical characteristic peaks for the elements Fe, Na, S, O, and C is provided (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0004">4c</a>). The C 1s of the NFS/KB at 284&nbsp;eV were further analyzed and the high-resolution plot is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0004">4d</a>. The four peaks at 284.9, 285.8, 287.3 and 289.4&nbsp;eV correspond to the C─C, C─O, CO, and O─C─O bonds,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0040_R_d93665352e879" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nano Energy">40</a>, <a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0041_R_d93665352e879" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively, presenting typical functional groups of conductive carbon, which ensures the excellent electronic conductivity of the composite. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0004">4e</a> displays high-resolution fitted curves of Fe 2p in NFS/KB. The Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> peak is at 711&nbsp;eV and the Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub> peak is at 724&nbsp;eV, with corresponding satellite peaks at 715.2&nbsp;eV and 729.1&nbsp;eV, indicating that the valence of Fe in NFS/KB is +2.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0042_R_d93665352e892" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Energy Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fe 2p and C 1s spectra of NFS/AB (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a,c</a>, Supporting Information) and NFS/SP (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b,d</a>, Supporting Information) are also presented. The fitted results of their Fe 2p spectra are consistent with that of NFS/KB, indicating the Fe in NFS/AB and NFS/SP are all +2. Importantly, the C 1s spectra of the three samples are different. The results of deconvolution of the C 1s peaks of the three samples are shown in Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). The types of oxygen-containing functional groups of the three samples are mainly C─O, C═O, and O─C═O. The samples exhibited good air stability as they all displayed the highest carbonyl content of the hydrophobic functional group and the lowest carboxyl content of the hydrophilic functional group. Specifically, NFS/KB has a relatively higher carboxyl content, suggesting that it may promote electrolyte ion transport to some degree.</p>
         
         <p>We also tested the XPS of NFS/AB and NFS/SP, and the results obtained for the split peaks C 1s and Fe<sup>2+</sup> are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information). The fitted results for the split peaks of C 1s and Fe<sup>2+</sup> for NFS/AB and NFS/SP were consistent with those of NFS/KB.</p>
         
         <p>The specific carbon content and thermal stability of all the samples were investigated by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) at the temperature range from 30 to 800 °C in air atmosphere (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). In the range of 30&nbsp;°C−200&nbsp;°C, a slight drop in quality within 1% could be observed due to the evaporation of absorbed water or crystal water.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0043_R_d93665352e924" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Power Sources">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> There is a slight climb in the weight of NFS at ≈350 °C, which is due to the oxidation of Fe<sup>2+</sup>. The TGA curve of NFS is basically straight in the range of 450−550 °C, because the weight loss is in dynamic equilibrium due to the oxidation of Fe<sup>2+</sup> and the decomposition of sulfate. A sharp decline with an inflexion at 550 °C can be detected, which can be attributed to the further decomposition of NFS.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0044_R_d93665352e934" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The NFS/KB sample shows the similar TGA curve of NFS, apart from a mass loss within 400 to 500 °C, which is related to the carbon content in this composite. Therefore, the carbon content in NFS/KB can be determined by the difference in mass loss between the two samples of NFS/KB and NFS, thus the carbon content in NFS/KB is ≈11% (a-b = 11%).</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performances of NFS/C half-cells were initially examined, and the first-cycle CV curves of NFS/AB, NFS/KB, and NFS/SP are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0005">5a</a>. Among the NFS/C samples, the NFS/KB exhibits higher peak intensities, larger integration area, and smaller polarization owing to its uniform and continuous conductive network structure. And the sharp oxidation peaks for all samples located at ≈4.2&nbsp;V in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0005">5a</a> are as well associated with the irreversible insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> at the Na1 position. The redox peaks located at ≈3.46/3.37 and 3.87/3.71&nbsp;V correspond to the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/disinsertion to Na2 and Na3 positions.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202402145-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6356f762-9cae-41a0-af9d-c62a19ce638f/adfm202402145-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6356f762-9cae-41a0-af9d-c62a19ce638f/adfm202402145-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/440c5c33-2ff2-4bf7-ada3-632d761159f7/adfm202402145-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6356f762-9cae-41a0-af9d-c62a19ce638f/adfm202402145-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202402145-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202402145" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performances of NFS/C as cathodes: a) First-turn CV profiles at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> of NFS/C. b) Rate capability at different rates. Cycling stability at c)1 C and d) 20 C. e) EIS analysis. f) Impedance versus frequency curves. g) First three charge-discharge curves. h) CV profiles at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> of NFS/KB.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">Moreover, all the samples based on the constructed dual-carbon structure exhibit excellent rate performance, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0005">5b</a>. Especially, NFS/KB possesses a reversible capacity of 92 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C with a capacity of 90 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> when the current returns 0.1 C after high-rate cycling. Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information) shows the charge/discharge cycle curves of NFS/KB at different current densities. The voltage plateau does not show a significant decrease with increasing current density, exhibiting good rate performance. The cycling performance of NFS/C was tested at different currents to examine the cycling stability of the material. Satisfyingly, the NFS/KB exhibits good cycling stability at 1 C (with 95% capacity retention after 100 cycles) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0005">5c</a>), which is mainly because the NFS nanoparticles are dispersed in the carbon network structure of ketjen black to improve the electronic and ionic conductivity. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0005">5d</a>, the NFS/KB exhibited a maximum reversible capacity of 67 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a capacity retention rate of 85% after 500 cycles at 20 C. And the coulombic efficiencies for all samples are close to 100% in both low and high current (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information) cycles, which mainly derive from the higher structural stability and the excellent ionic conductivity. Moreover, the electrochemical impedance tests (EIS) were carried out to initially evaluate the conductivity and kinetic performance of the NFS/C cathode half-cells and the results are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0005">5e,f</a>. Obviously, the NFS/KB has the lowest charge transfer resistance and diffusion resistance, mainly due to that the in situ carbon coating layer improves rapid electronic and ionic path. And the uniform Na<sub>2.31</sub>Fe<sub>1.72</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> particles are embedded in the continuous ketjen black carbon networks, which are more favorable for diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup>. And the relevant diffusion coefficient for Na<sup>+</sup> (<i>D<sub>Na+</sub></i>) is calculated as follows:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0045_R_d93665352e1025" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">45</a>, <a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0046_R_d93665352e1025" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - J. Phys. Chem. C">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202402145-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>where R, T, A, n, F, C, and σ represent the gas constant, temperature, surface area, number of electrons transferred during the reaction, Faraday's constant, Na<sup>+</sup> concentration, and Warburg's constant, respectively, and σ is the slope of Z' to ω <sup>−1/2</sup>. All calculations are shown in Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information). NFS/KB exhibits the smallest Rct value (157.8 Ω), proving the rapid kinetic behavior of the material. The <i>D<sub>Na+</sub></i> of NFS/KB is 1.68 × 10<sup>−15</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, which possesses a faster ionic diffusion compared to the other two samples.</div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Based on the promising performance of NFS/KB, further analysis of its electrochemical performance was performed. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0005">5g</a> shows the first three charge/discharge curves of NFS/KB at 0.1 C. The low initial coulombic efficiency is mainly associated with the irreversible insertion/disinsertion behavior of Na<sup>+</sup>, which is similar to the structural characteristics embodied in the Li<sub>2</sub>FeSiO<sub>4</sub> and Li<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> materials reported previously in the literature,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0047_R_d93665352e1145" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Chem. Mater.">47</a>, <a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0048_R_d93665352e1145" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Nat. Commun.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> but a stable structure of NFS/KB will be formed in the subsequent cycles and exhibits excellent capacity reversibility. As seen in the CV diagram (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0005">5h</a>), after the formation of a stable Na<sup>+</sup> transport channel in the first cycle, the CV curves of the last two cycles showed a stable overlap, reflecting good capacity reversibility. What's more, a deeper analysis of the kinetic behavior was performed by testing the CV of NFS/KB at different scan rates, and the CV curves at 0.1 to 0.4&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a>, Supporting Information) exhibited similar shapes further demonstrating the good reversibility of the electrochemical reaction of NFS/KB with increasing scan rates. Both surface-controlled and diffusion-controlled reaction are included in the charge storage mechanism for Na<sup>+</sup> storage. According to the relationship between the measured current (i) and the scan rate (ν):<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0049_R_d93665352e1163" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202402145-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/a0b42cf1-f463-441e-b4b7-18692929bf30/adfm202402145-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202402145:adfm202402145-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202402145-math-0003.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>i</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mi>a</mi>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>v</mi>
                              <mi>b</mi>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation} i=a{v}^{b} \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The b value can be determined from the slope of the log(v)-log(i) curve. In general, when the b value is equal to 0.5, the electrochemical process is a behavior fully controlled by diffusion-controlled process, while the b value of 1.0 indicates control by surface-controlled process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0050_R_d93665352e1185" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Power Sources">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Evidently, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b</a> (Supporting Information), the b values of peaks group 1–6 are 1.05, 0.99, 0.86, 1.0, 1.05, and 0.82, respectively, indicating that the electrochemical reactions of the NFS/KB are dominated by the surface-controlled process. Based on the equation:

            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202402145-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/36539336-50b9-4eb7-b1e3-d7c31f988240/adfm202402145-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202402145:adfm202402145-math-0004" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202402145-math-0004.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>i</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>k</mi>
                              <mn>1</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <mi>v</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>k</mi>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>v</mi>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mo>−</mo>
                                 <mn>1</mn>
                                 <mo>/</mo>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </mrow>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation} i={k}_{1}v+{k}_{2}{v}^{-1/2} \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>The quantitative capacity contribution of the surface control can be obtained. Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10c</a> (Supporting Information) details the contribution of the surface-controlled capacity at 0.3&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> and calculates a contribution of 95% for the orange region. The high pseudo-capacitance contribution is mainly due to the smaller particle size of NFS/KB, which provides more active sites to exhibit higher kinetics. And the quantitative data (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10d</a>, Supporting Information) suggested that the capacity controlled by the surface behavior gradually increased with increasing scan rate, reaching 96% at 0.4&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, indicating that surface behavior dominated the Na<sup>+</sup> storage process at high scan rates and facilitated the acquisition of higher-rate performance.</p>
         
         <p>With the excellent performance of the cathode, we further explore the electrochemical performance of NFS/KB materials applied to the anode. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0006">6a</a> shows the CV results for the NFS/KB anode at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The sharp reduction peak near 0.25&nbsp;V in the first cycle is associated with the formation of the SEI film,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0051_R_d93665352e1247" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - J. Power Sources">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the position of the redox peak stabilizes near 2.25/1.5&nbsp;V in the subsequent cycles, which corresponds to the Fe<sup>2+</sup>↔Fe<sup>0</sup> conversion, which is like the reported results for the FeO anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402145-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402145-bib-0052_R_d93665352e1257" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To verify the Fe<sup>2+</sup>↔Fe<sup>0</sup> conversion, XPS test on the NFS/KB anode material discharged to 0.01&nbsp;V is also provided. The Fe 2p<sup>3/2</sup> spectra of the NFS-KB cathode and the NFS-KB anode are deconvolved in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information). Compared to the NFS-KB cathode, two distinct peaks can be observed in the NFS-KB anode, representing Fe<sup>2+</sup> at ≈711&nbsp;eV and Fe<sup>0</sup> at ≈707&nbsp;eV, further indicating the charge-discharge plateaus at 2.25/1.5&nbsp;V correspond to the conversion of Fe<sup>2+</sup> to Fe<sup>0</sup>. The first three charge/discharge cycle curves for the NFS/KB anode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0006">6b</a>) exhibit the similar results corresponding to the CV curves, showing that all charging plateaus stabilize ≈2.25&nbsp;V after the first cycle. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0006">6c</a> shows the rate performance of the NFS/KB anode at the currents of 0.6, 1, 3, 5, and 10 C, which reveal a reversible capacity of 195, 186, 172, 165, and 149 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Surprisingly, the rate performance exhibited at high current densities is even better than that reported in the literature for hard carbon anode, and the comparative results are shown in Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information). In addition, a reversible capacity of 190 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is still available after a high current cycle and then back to a low current of 0.3 C, demonstrating good capacity reversibility.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202402145-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/590a7778-2207-4124-8eea-f963b97f3b1c/adfm202402145-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/590a7778-2207-4124-8eea-f963b97f3b1c/adfm202402145-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1e6bfb55-c0e8-417a-b43a-eec2267bcfb0/adfm202402145-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/590a7778-2207-4124-8eea-f963b97f3b1c/adfm202402145-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202402145-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202402145" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performances of NFS/KB as anodes: a) First three cycles of CV curves at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) First three charge-discharge curves. c) Rate performance. d) cycling stability at 10 C up to 500 cycles.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The cycling performance of the pre-activated NFS/KB anode was further tested at 10 C. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0006">6d</a>, a reversible capacity of 153 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> could still be maintained after 500 cycles, and the coulombic efficiency is close to 100%, exhibiting good cycling stability. Then the kinetic behavior of NFS/KB anode was analyzed by CV tests at different scan rates. Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a</a> (Supporting Information) shows the CV results at 0.1–0.4&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating similar results to the cathode. The calculated b-values (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12b</a>, Supporting Information) show that surface-controlled and diffusion-controlled processes co-control their kinetic behavior. Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12c</a> (Supporting Information) details the contribution of the surface control capacity at 0.3&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> and calculates a contribution of 50% for the orange region. The quantitative data (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12d</a>, Supporting Information) show that the capacity controlled by the surface-controlled gradually increases with increasing scan rate, reaching 53% at 0.4&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. According to the results above, the NFS/KB material can also exhibit excellent electrochemical performance as anode, which lays the foundation for the application of this material to symmetric sodium ion full cells.</p>
         
         <p>According to the excellent electrochemical performance of NFS/KB composites in the cathode and anode of SIBs, the NFS/KB materials were assembled into symmetric full cells as cathode and anode for deeper development of the material in practical production. The model diagram of the full cell is shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0007">7a</a>, where NFS/KB is used as both cathode and anode materials. The rate performance testing showed excellent reversible capacity at high and low currents (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0007">7b</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information), with good cycling stability at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, and 2 C with reversible capacities of 87, 80, 76, 72, and 70 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> respectively. When returning to a low current of 0.1 C after high-current cycling, a reversible capacity of 85 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is obtained, indicating an excellent structural stability. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0007">7c</a> shows that the first three charge and discharge cycle curves almost overlapped, which demonstrates the symmetric full cell can effectively reduce the irreversibility and polarization during the initial electrochemical process. The cycling performance of the full cell was then tested at 0.2 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402145-fig-0007">7d</a>), exhibiting a reversible capacity of up to 75 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which provides an advanced idea for the development of sodium ion symmetric full cells.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202402145-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9d20becf-2912-4f80-a123-98ad6053cdc1/adfm202402145-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9d20becf-2912-4f80-a123-98ad6053cdc1/adfm202402145-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/448b641f-50fa-478e-b21f-c70b33b5b0c2/adfm202402145-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9d20becf-2912-4f80-a123-98ad6053cdc1/adfm202402145-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202402145-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202402145" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performances of symmetrical full cell NFS/KB//NFS/KB: a) Model schematic illustration; b) rate performance; c) first three cycles charge-discharge curves after pre-activation and d) cycling stability up to 50 cycles at 0.2 C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202402145-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202402145-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we prepared carbon-coated Na<sub>2.31</sub>Fe<sub>1.72</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> particles embedded in the continuous conductive carbon networks with a dual-conductive structure via a slow diffusion solid phase ball-milling strategy. Within the constructed dual-carbon structure, the uniform continuous carbon network improves the intrinsic electronic conductivity and ion transport efficiency among particles of the material. In addition, the uniformly dispersed small-sized particles with abundant active sites can fully contact with the electrolyte, resulting in excellent rate performance and cycling stability. More significantly, the material delivers superior rate performance to hard carbon in the anode of SIBs. Based on the favorable electrochemical performance of the material at the anode and cathode of SIBs, the assembled symmetric sodium-ion full cells with NFS/KB material also exhibit promising electrochemical performance. This work provides a facile synthesis method and a low-cost carbon modification strategy for Fe-based sulphate materials and presents a new idea for the development of low-cost anode for SIBs, as well as a novel insight into the design of new symmetric sodium ion full cells.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
